<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Scientific study of the composition, structure, and history of Earth</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Earth science. For the scientific journal, see <a href="/wiki/Geology_(journal)" title="Geology (journal)"><i>Geology</i> (journal)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Geology</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%E1%BF%86#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:γῆ">γῆ</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>gê</i></span>)</i> 'earth', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-%CE%BBo%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:-λoγία">-λoγία</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i><a href="/wiki/-logy" title="-logy">-logía</a></i></span>)</i> 'study of, discourse')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ-2">[2]</a></sup> is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a> concerned with <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical objects</a>, the features or <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a> of which it is composed, and the processes by which they change over time. Modern geology significantly overlaps all other <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth sciences</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a>, and so is treated as one major aspect of integrated <a href="/wiki/Earth_system_science" title="Earth system science">Earth system science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_science" title="Planetary science">planetary science</a>.
</p><p>Geology describes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">structure of the Earth</a> on and beneath its surface, and the processes that have shaped that structure. It also provides tools to determine the <a href="/wiki/Relative_dating" title="Relative dating">relative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">absolute ages</a> of rocks found in a given location, and also to describe the histories of those rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> By combining these tools, <a href="/wiki/Geologist" title="Geologist">geologists</a> are able to chronicle the geological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" title="History of the Earth">history of the Earth</a> as a whole, and also to demonstrate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a>. Geology provides the primary evidence for <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history of life</a>, and the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">past climates</a>.
</p><p>Geologists use a wide variety of methods to understand the Earth's structure and evolution, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_work" title="Field work">field work</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petrology" title="Petrology">rock description</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_survey" title="Geophysical survey">geophysical techniques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">chemical analysis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_experiment" title="Physical experiment">physical experiments</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">numerical modelling</a>. In practical terms, geology is important for <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mineral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_geology" title="Petroleum geology">hydrocarbon</a> exploration and exploitation, evaluating <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">water resources</a>, understanding of <a href="/wiki/Natural_hazard" title="Natural hazard">natural hazards</a>, the remediation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_Geology" title="Environmental Geology">environmental</a> problems, and providing insights into past <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">climate change</a>. Geology is a major <a href="/wiki/Academic_discipline" title="Academic discipline">academic discipline</a>, and it is central to <a href="/wiki/Geological_engineering" title="Geological engineering">geological engineering</a> and plays an important role in <a href="/wiki/Geotechnical_engineering" title="Geotechnical engineering">geotechnical engineering</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_material">Geological material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geological material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The majority of geological data comes from research on solid Earth materials. Meteorites and other extraterrestrial natural materials are also studied by geological methods.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral">Mineral</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mineral">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Mineral</a></div>
<p>Minerals are natural occurring elements and compounds with a definite homogeneous chemical composition and ordered atomic composition.
</p><p>Each mineral has distinct physical properties, and there are many tests to determine each of them. The specimens can be tested for:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Luster</i>: Quality of light reflected from the surface of a mineral. Examples are metallic, pearly, waxy, dull.</li>
<li><i>Color</i>: Minerals are grouped by their color. Mostly diagnostic but impurities can change a mineral's color.</li>
<li>Streak: Performed by scratching the sample on a <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> plate. The color of the streak can help name the mineral.</li>
<li>Hardness: The resistance of a mineral to scratching.</li>
<li>Breakage pattern: A mineral can either show fracture or cleavage, the former being breakage of uneven surfaces, and the latter a breakage along closely spaced parallel planes.</li>
<li>Specific gravity: the weight of a specific volume of a mineral.</li>
<li>Effervescence: Involves dripping <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> on the mineral to test for fizzing.</li>
<li>Magnetism: Involves using a magnet to test for <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a>.</li>
<li>Taste: Minerals can have a distinctive taste, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halite_(mineral)" title="Halite (mineral)">halite</a> (which tastes like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Table_salt" title="Table salt">table salt</a>).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock">Rock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rockcycle_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1608" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rockcycle_edit.jpg/220px-Rockcycle_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rockcycle_edit.jpg/330px-Rockcycle_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Rockcycle_edit.jpg/440px-Rockcycle_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rockcycle_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rock_cycle" title="Rock cycle">rock cycle</a> shows the relationship between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">igneous</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metamorphic" title="Metamorphic">metamorphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">Rock (geology)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_cycle" title="Rock cycle">Rock cycle</a></div>
<p>A rock is any naturally occurring solid mass or aggregate of minerals or <a href="/wiki/Mineraloid" title="Mineraloid">mineraloids</a>. Most research in geology is associated with the study of rocks, as they provide the primary record of the majority of the geological history of the Earth. There are three major types of rock: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">igneous</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary" title="Sedimentary">sedimentary</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metamorphic" title="Metamorphic">metamorphic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rock_cycle" title="Rock cycle">rock cycle</a> 
illustrates the relationships among them (see diagram).
</p><p>When a rock <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solidifies" title="Solidifies">solidifies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallizes</a> from melt (<a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a>), it is an igneous rock. This rock can be <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathered</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eroded" title="Eroded">eroded</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">redeposited</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithification" title="Lithification">lithified</a> into a sedimentary rock. It can then be turned into a <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic rock</a> by heat and pressure that change its <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> content, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Fabric_(geology)" title="Fabric (geology)">characteristic fabric</a>. All three types may melt again, and when this happens, new magma is formed, from which an igneous rock may once more solidify.
Organic matter, such as coal, bitumen, oil and natural gas, is linked mainly to organic-rich sedimentary rocks.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Or_Venezuela.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2205" data-file-width="2106" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Or_Venezuela.jpg/220px-Or_Venezuela.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Or_Venezuela.jpg/330px-Or_Venezuela.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Or_Venezuela.jpg/440px-Or_Venezuela.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Or_Venezuela.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">Native</a> <a href="/wiki/Gold#Occurrence" title="Gold">gold</a> from <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quartz,_Tibet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1596" data-file-width="1592" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg/220px-Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg/330px-Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg/440px-Quartz%2C_Tibet.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quartz,_Tibet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">Quartz</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>. Quartz makes up more than 10% of the Earth's crust by mass.</div></div></div>
<p>To study all three types of rock, geologists evaluate the minerals of which they are composed and their other physical properties, such as <a href="/wiki/Texture_(geology)" title="Texture (geology)">texture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fabric_(geology)" title="Fabric (geology)">fabric</a>. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unlithified_material">Unlithified material</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Unlithified material">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Geologists also study unlithified materials (referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Superficial_deposits" title="Superficial deposits">superficial deposits</a></i>) that lie above the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This study is often known as <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_geology" title="Quaternary geology">Quaternary geology</a>, after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaternary_period" title="Quaternary period">Quaternary period</a> of geologic history, which is the most recent period of geologic time.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magma">Magma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Magma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">Magma</a></div>
<p>Magma is the original unlithified source of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous_rocks" title="Igneous rocks">igneous rocks</a>. The active flow of molten rock is closely studied in <a href="/wiki/Volcanology" title="Volcanology">volcanology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Igneous_petrology" title="Igneous petrology">igneous petrology</a> aims to determine the history of igneous rocks from their original molten source to their final crystallization.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Whole-Earth_structure">Whole-Earth structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Whole-Earth structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">Plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">Plate tectonics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Active_Margin.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="171" data-file-width="305" decoding="async" height="123" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Active_Margin.svg/220px-Active_Margin.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Active_Margin.svg/330px-Active_Margin.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Active_Margin.svg/440px-Active_Margin.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Active_Margin.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oceanic-continental convergence resulting in <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_arc" title="Volcanic arc">volcanic arcs</a> illustrates one effect of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2844" data-file-width="4167" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/300px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/450px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Plates_tect2_en.svg/600px-Plates_tect2_en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plates_tect2_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plates" title="Tectonic plates">tectonic plates</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1960s, it was discovered that the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> and rigid uppermost portion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_mantle" title="Upper mantle">upper mantle</a>, is separated into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plate" title="Tectonic plate">tectonic plates</a> that move across the <a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">plastically</a> deforming, solid, upper mantle, which is called the <a href="/wiki/Asthenosphere" title="Asthenosphere">asthenosphere</a>. This theory is supported by several types of observations, including seafloor spreading<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TDE_discovery_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-TDE_discovery-7">[7]</a></sup> and the global distribution of mountain terrain and seismicity.
</p><p>There is an intimate coupling between the movement of the plates on the surface and the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_convection" title="Mantle convection">convection of the mantle</a> (that is, the heat transfer caused by the slow movement of ductile mantle rock). Thus, oceanic plates and the adjoining mantle <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection currents</a> always move in the same direction – because the oceanic lithosphere is actually the rigid upper thermal <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer" title="Boundary layer">boundary layer</a> of the convecting mantle. This coupling between rigid plates moving on the surface of the Earth and the convecting <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is called plate tectonics.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Farallon_Plate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="647" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Farallon_Plate.jpg/300px-Farallon_Plate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Farallon_Plate.jpg/450px-Farallon_Plate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Farallon_Plate.jpg/600px-Farallon_Plate.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Farallon_Plate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this diagram based on <a href="/wiki/Seismic_tomography" title="Seismic tomography">seismic tomography</a>, subducting <a href="/wiki/Slab_(geology)" title="Slab (geology)">slabs</a> are in blue and continental margins and a few plate boundaries are in red. The blue blob in the cutaway section is the <a href="/wiki/Farallon_Plate" title="Farallon Plate">Farallon Plate</a>, which is subducting beneath North America. The remnants of this plate on the surface of the Earth are the <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_Fuca_Plate" title="Juan de Fuca Plate">Juan de Fuca Plate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Explorer_Plate" title="Explorer Plate">Explorer Plate</a>, both in the northwestern United States and southwestern Canada and the <a href="/wiki/Cocos_Plate" title="Cocos Plate">Cocos Plate</a> on the west coast of Mexico.</div></div></div>
<p>The development of plate tectonics has provided a physical basis for many observations of the solid Earth. Long linear regions of geological features are explained as plate boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TDE_plates_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-TDE_plates-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<p>For example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">Mid-ocean ridges</a>, high regions on the seafloor where <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a> and volcanoes exist, are seen as <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundaries</a>, where two plates move apart.</li>
<li>Arcs of volcanoes and earthquakes are theorized as <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent boundaries</a>, where one plate <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subducts</a>, or moves, under another.</li></ul>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transform_boundary" title="Transform boundary">Transform boundaries</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/San_Andreas_Fault" title="San Andreas Fault">San Andreas Fault</a> system, resulted in widespread powerful earthquakes. Plate tectonics also has provided a mechanism for <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Wegener" title="Alfred Wegener">Alfred Wegener</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> in which the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continents" title="Continents">continents</a> move across the surface of the Earth over geological time. They also provided a driving force for crustal deformation, and a new setting for the observations of structural geology. The power of the theory of plate tectonics lies in its ability to combine all of these observations into a single theory of how the lithosphere moves over the convecting mantle.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth_structure">Earth structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Earth structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">Structure of the Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jordens_inre-numbers.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="833" data-file-width="875" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Jordens_inre-numbers.svg/220px-Jordens_inre-numbers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Jordens_inre-numbers.svg/330px-Jordens_inre-numbers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Jordens_inre-numbers.svg/440px-Jordens_inre-numbers.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jordens_inre-numbers.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s layered structure. (1) inner core; (2) outer core; (3) lower mantle; (4) upper mantle; (5) lithosphere; (6) crust (part of the lithosphere)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earthquake_wave_paths.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Earthquake_wave_paths.svg/300px-Earthquake_wave_paths.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Earthquake_wave_paths.svg/450px-Earthquake_wave_paths.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Earthquake_wave_paths.svg/600px-Earthquake_wave_paths.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earthquake_wave_paths.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Earth layered structure. Typical wave paths from earthquakes like these gave early seismologists insights into the layered structure of the Earth</div></div></div>
<p>Advances in <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_modeling" title="Computer modeling">computer modeling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">mineralogy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallography</a> at high temperatures and pressures give insights into the internal composition and structure of the Earth.
</p><p>Seismologists can use the arrival times of <a href="/wiki/Seismic_wave" title="Seismic wave">seismic waves</a> to image the interior of the Earth. Early advances in this field showed the existence of a liquid <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_outer_core" title="Earth's outer core">outer core</a> (where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-wave" title="S-wave">shear waves</a> were not able to propagate) and a dense solid <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_inner_core" title="Earth's inner core">inner core</a>. These advances led to the development of a layered model of the Earth, with a <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> on top, the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> below (separated within itself by <a href="/wiki/Seismic_tomography" title="Seismic tomography">seismic discontinuities</a> at 410 and 660 kilometers), and the outer core and inner core below that. More recently, seismologists have been able to create detailed images of wave speeds inside the earth in the same way a doctor images a body in a CT scan. These images have led to a much more detailed view of the interior of the Earth, and have replaced the simplified layered model with a much more dynamic model.
</p><p>Mineralogists have been able to use the pressure and temperature data from the seismic and modeling studies alongside knowledge of the elemental composition of the Earth to reproduce these conditions in experimental settings and measure changes in crystal structure. These studies explain the chemical changes associated with the major seismic discontinuities in the mantle and show the crystallographic structures expected in the inner core of the Earth.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_time">Geological time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geological time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">Geological history of Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">Geologic time scale</a></div>
<p>The geological time scale encompasses the history of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It is bracketed at the earliest by the dates of the first <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> material at 4.567 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigaannum" title="Gigaannum">Ga</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.567_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-4.567-11">[11]</a></sup> (or 4.567 billion years ago) and the formation of the Earth at
4.54 Ga<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.54_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-4.54-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.54_book_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-4.54_book-13">[13]</a></sup>
(4.54 billion years), which is the beginning of the informally recognized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadean_eon" title="Hadean eon">Hadean eon</a> – a division of geological time. At the later end of the scale, it is marked by the present day (in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holocene_epoch" title="Holocene epoch">Holocene epoch</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timescale_of_the_Earth">Timescale of the Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Timescale of the Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following five timelines show the geologic time scale to scale. The first shows the entire time from the formation of the Earth to the present, but this gives little space for the most recent eon. The second timeline shows an expanded view of the most recent eon. In a similar way, the most recent era is expanded in the third timeline, the most recent period is expanded in the fourth timeline, and the most recent epoch is expanded in the fifth timeline.
</p>

<small>Millions of Years (1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th)<br/>Thousands of years (5th)</small></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_milestones_on_Earth">Important milestones on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Important milestones on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="491" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg/300px-Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg/450px-Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg/600px-Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geologic_Clock_with_events_and_periods.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geological time in a diagram called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_clock" title="Geological clock">geological clock</a>, showing the relative lengths of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_eon" title="Geologic eon">eons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Era_(geology)" title="Era (geology)">eras</a> of the Earth's history</div></div></div>
<ul><li>4.567 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ga_(unit)" title="Ga (unit)">Ga</a> (gigaannum: billion years ago): <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Solar system formation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.567_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-4.567-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>4.54 Ga: <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">Accretion, or formation</a>, of Earth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.54_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-4.54-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.54_book_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-4.54_book-13">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 4 Ga: End of <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a>, the first life</li>
<li>c. 3.5 Ga: Start of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a></li>
<li>c. 2.3 Ga: Oxygenated <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, first <a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">snowball Earth</a></li>
<li>730–635 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma_(unit)" title="Ma (unit)">Ma</a> (megaannum: million years ago): second snowball Earth</li>
<li>541 ± 0.3 Ma: <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a> – vast multiplication of hard-bodied life; first abundant <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a>; start of the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a></li>
<li>c. 380 Ma: First <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> land animals</li>
<li>250 Ma: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Permian-Triassic_extinction" title="Permian-Triassic extinction">Permian-Triassic extinction</a> – 90% of all land animals die; end of Paleozoic and beginning of <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a></li>
<li>66 Ma: <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction</a> – <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">Dinosaurs</a> die; end of Mesozoic and beginning of <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a></li>
<li>c. 7 Ma: First <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominin" title="Hominin">hominins</a> appear</li>
<li>3.9 Ma: First <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a>, direct ancestor to modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a>, appear</li>
<li>200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ma_(unit)" title="Ma (unit)">ka</a> (kiloannum: thousand years ago): First modern Homo sapiens appear in East Africa</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timescale_of_the_Moon">Timescale of the Moon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Timescale of the Moon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lunar_geologic_timescale" title="Lunar geologic timescale">Lunar geologic timescale</a></div>

<p><br/>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timescale_of_Mars">Timescale of Mars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Timescale of Mars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dating_methods">Dating methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Dating methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relative_dating">Relative dating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Relative dating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Relative_dating" title="Relative dating">Relative dating</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross-cutting_relations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cross-cutting_relations.svg/300px-Cross-cutting_relations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cross-cutting_relations.svg/450px-Cross-cutting_relations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Cross-cutting_relations.svg/600px-Cross-cutting_relations.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross-cutting_relations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-cutting_relations" title="Cross-cutting relations">Cross-cutting relations</a> can be used to determine the relative ages of <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">rock strata</a> and other geological structures. Explanations: A – <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folded</a> rock strata cut by a <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust fault</a>; B – large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrusion" title="Intrusion">intrusion</a> (cutting through A); C – <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosional</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angular_unconformity" title="Angular unconformity">angular unconformity</a> (cutting off A &amp; B) on which rock strata were deposited; D – <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">volcanic dyke</a> (cutting through A, B &amp; C); E – even younger rock strata (overlying C &amp; D); F – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_fault" title="Normal fault">normal fault</a> (cutting through A, B, C &amp; E).</div></div></div>
<p>Methods for <a href="/wiki/Relative_dating" title="Relative dating">relative dating</a> were developed when geology first emerged as a <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>. Geologists still use the following principles today as a means to provide information about geological history and the timing of geological events.
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principle_of_uniformitarianism" title="Principle of uniformitarianism">principle of uniformitarianism</a></i> states that the geological processes observed in operation that modify the Earth's crust at present have worked in much the same way over geological time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> A fundamental principle of geology advanced by the 18th-century Scottish physician and geologist <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a> is that "the present is the key to the past." In Hutton's words: "the past history of our globe must be explained by what can be seen to be happening now."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intrusion_(geology)" title="Intrusion (geology)">principle of intrusive relationships</a></i> concerns crosscutting intrusions. In geology, when an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous_rocks" title="Igneous rocks">igneous</a> intrusion cuts across a formation of <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rock</a>, it can be determined that the igneous intrusion is younger than the sedimentary rock. Different types of intrusions include stocks, <a href="/wiki/Laccolith" title="Laccolith">laccoliths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batholith" title="Batholith">batholiths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">sills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">dikes</a>.
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principle_of_cross-cutting_relationships" title="Principle of cross-cutting relationships">principle of cross-cutting relationships</a></i> pertains to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faults</a> and the age of the sequences through which they cut. Faults are younger than the rocks they cut; accordingly, if a fault is found that penetrates some formations but not those on top of it, then the formations that were cut are older than the fault, and the ones that are not cut must be younger than the fault. Finding the key bed in these situations may help determine whether the fault is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_fault" title="Normal fault">normal fault</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Thrust_fault" title="Thrust fault">thrust fault</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steno_strat_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-steno_strat-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Principle_of_inclusions_and_components" title="Principle of inclusions and components">principle of inclusions and components</a></i> states that, with sedimentary rocks, if inclusions (or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clastic_rocks" title="Clastic rocks">clasts</a></i>) are found in a formation, then the inclusions must be older than the formation that contains them. For example, in sedimentary rocks, it is common for gravel from an older formation to be ripped up and included in a newer layer. A similar situation with igneous rocks occurs when <a href="/wiki/Xenolith" title="Xenolith">xenoliths</a> are found. These foreign bodies are picked up as <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> or lava flows, and are incorporated, later to cool in the matrix. As a result, xenoliths are older than the rock that contains them.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SEUtahStrat.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1794" data-file-width="3161" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/SEUtahStrat.JPG/300px-SEUtahStrat.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/SEUtahStrat.JPG/450px-SEUtahStrat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/SEUtahStrat.JPG/600px-SEUtahStrat.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SEUtahStrat.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">Permian</a> through <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> stratigraphy of the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Plateau" title="Colorado Plateau">Colorado Plateau</a> area of southeastern <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> is an example of both original horizontality and the law of superposition. These strata make up much of the famous prominent rock formations in widely spaced protected areas such as <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Reef_National_Park" title="Capitol Reef National Park">Capitol Reef National Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canyonlands_National_Park" title="Canyonlands National Park">Canyonlands National Park</a>. From top to bottom: Rounded tan domes of the <a href="/wiki/Navajo_Sandstone" title="Navajo Sandstone">Navajo Sandstone</a>, layered red <a href="/wiki/Kayenta_Formation" title="Kayenta Formation">Kayenta Formation</a>, cliff-forming, vertically jointed, red <a href="/wiki/Wingate_Sandstone" title="Wingate Sandstone">Wingate Sandstone</a>, slope-forming, purplish <a href="/wiki/Chinle_Formation" title="Chinle Formation">Chinle Formation</a>, layered, lighter-red <a href="/wiki/Moenkopi_Formation" title="Moenkopi Formation">Moenkopi Formation</a>, and white, layered <a href="/wiki/Cutler_Formation" title="Cutler Formation">Cutler Formation</a> sandstone. Picture from <a href="/wiki/Glen_Canyon_National_Recreation_Area" title="Glen Canyon National Recreation Area">Glen Canyon National Recreation Area</a>, Utah.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Principle_of_original_horizontality" title="Principle of original horizontality">principle of original horizontality</a></i> states that the deposition of sediments occurs as essentially horizontal beds. Observation of modern marine and non-marine sediments in a wide variety of environments supports this generalization (although <a href="/wiki/Cross-bedding" title="Cross-bedding">cross-bedding</a> is inclined, the overall orientation of cross-bedded units is horizontal).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steno_strat_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-steno_strat-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Law_of_superposition" title="Law of superposition">principle of superposition</a></i> states that a sedimentary rock layer in a <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonically</a> undisturbed sequence is younger than the one beneath it and older than the one above it. Logically a younger layer cannot slip beneath a layer previously deposited. This principle allows sedimentary layers to be viewed as a form of the vertical timeline, a partial or complete record of the time elapsed from deposition of the lowest layer to deposition of the highest bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-steno_strat_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-steno_strat-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Principle_of_faunal_succession" title="Principle of faunal succession">principle of faunal succession</a></i> is based on the appearance of fossils in sedimentary rocks. As organisms exist during the same period throughout the world, their presence or (sometimes) absence provides a relative age of the formations where they appear. Based on principles that William Smith laid out almost a hundred years before the publication of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>, the principles of succession developed independently of evolutionary thought. The principle becomes quite complex, however, given the uncertainties of fossilization, localization of fossil types due to lateral changes in habitat (<a href="/wiki/Facies" title="Facies">facies</a> change in sedimentary strata), and that not all fossils formed globally at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absolute_dating">Absolute dating</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Absolute dating">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Absolute_dating" title="Absolute dating">Absolute dating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">geochronology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="999" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg/170px-Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg/255px-Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg/340px-Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zircon-tuc1001b.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> <a href="/wiki/Zircon" title="Zircon">zircon</a> is often used in <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Geologists also use methods to determine the absolute age of rock samples and geological events. These dates are useful on their own and may also be used in conjunction with relative dating methods or to calibrate relative methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the beginning of the 20th century, advancement in geological science was facilitated by the ability to obtain accurate absolute dates to geological events using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> and other methods. This changed the understanding of geological time. Previously, geologists could only use fossils and stratigraphic correlation to date sections of rock relative to one another. With isotopic dates, it became possible to assign <a href="/wiki/Absolute_dating" title="Absolute dating">absolute ages</a> to rock units, and these absolute dates could be applied to fossil sequences in which there was datable material, converting the old relative ages into new absolute ages.
</p><p>For many geological applications, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotope_ratio" title="Isotope ratio">isotope ratios</a> of radioactive elements are measured in minerals that give the amount of time that has passed since a rock passed through its particular <a href="/wiki/Closure_temperature" title="Closure temperature">closure temperature</a>, the point at which different radiometric isotopes stop diffusing into and out of the <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal lattice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> These are used in <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">geochronologic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermochronology" title="Thermochronology">thermochronologic</a> studies. Common methods include <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93lead_dating" title="Uranium–lead dating">uranium–lead dating</a>, <a href="/wiki/K%E2%80%93Ar_dating" title="K–Ar dating">potassium–argon dating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argon%E2%80%93argon_dating" title="Argon–argon dating">argon–argon dating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93thorium_dating" title="Uranium–thorium dating">uranium–thorium dating</a>. These methods are used for a variety of applications. Dating of <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> layers found within a stratigraphic sequence can provide absolute age data for sedimentary rock units that do not contain radioactive isotopes and calibrate relative dating techniques. These methods can also be used to determine ages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pluton" title="Pluton">pluton</a> emplacement.
Thermochemical techniques can be used to determine temperature profiles within the crust, the uplift of mountain ranges, and paleo-topography.
</p><p>Fractionation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthanide_series" title="Lanthanide series">lanthanide series</a> elements is used to compute ages since rocks were removed from the mantle.
</p><p>Other methods are used for more recent events. <a href="/wiki/Optically_stimulated_luminescence" title="Optically stimulated luminescence">Optically stimulated luminescence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_isotope#Natural" title="Cosmogenic isotope">cosmogenic radionuclide</a> dating are used to date surfaces and/or erosion rates. <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">Dendrochronology</a> can also be used for the dating of landscapes. <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">Radiocarbon dating</a> is used for geologically young materials containing <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic carbon</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_development_of_an_area">Geological development of an area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Geological development of an area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Volcanosed.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="288" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Volcanosed.svg/300px-Volcanosed.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Volcanosed.svg/450px-Volcanosed.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Volcanosed.svg/600px-Volcanosed.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Volcanosed.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An originally horizontal sequence of sedimentary rocks (in shades of tan) are affected by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Igneous" title="Igneous">igneous</a> activity. Deep below the surface is a <a href="/wiki/Magma_chamber" title="Magma chamber">magma chamber</a> and large associated igneous bodies. The magma chamber feeds the <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcano</a>, and sends offshoots of <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> that will later crystallize into dikes and sills. Magma also advances upwards to form <a href="/wiki/Intrusive_rock" title="Intrusive rock">intrusive igneous bodies</a>. The diagram illustrates both a <a href="/wiki/Cinder_cone" title="Cinder cone">cinder cone</a> volcano, which releases ash, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_volcano" title="Composite volcano">composite volcano</a>, which releases both lava and ash.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fault_types.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="416" decoding="async" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/200px-Fault_types.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/300px-Fault_types.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Fault_types.svg/400px-Fault_types.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fault_types.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the three types of faults.<br/> A. Strike-slip faults occur when rock units slide past one another.<br/> B. Normal faults occur when rocks are undergoing horizontal extension.<br/> C. Reverse (or thrust) faults occur when rocks are undergoing horizontal shortening.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Andreas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/San_Andreas.jpg/170px-San_Andreas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/San_Andreas.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Andreas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/San_Andreas_Fault" title="San Andreas Fault">San Andreas Fault</a> in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a></div></div></div>
<p>The geology of an area changes through time as rock units are deposited and inserted, and deformational processes change their shapes and locations.
</p><p>Rock units are first emplaced either by deposition onto the surface or intrusion into the <a href="/wiki/Country_rock_(geology)" title="Country rock (geology)">overlying rock</a>. Deposition can occur when sediments settle onto the surface of the Earth and later <a href="/wiki/Lithification" title="Lithification">lithify</a> into sedimentary rock, or when as <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_rock" title="Volcanic rock">volcanic material</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lava_flow" title="Lava flow">lava flows</a> blanket the surface. <a href="/wiki/Igneous_intrusion" title="Igneous intrusion">Igneous intrusions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Batholith" title="Batholith">batholiths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laccolith" title="Laccolith">laccoliths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">dikes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sill_(geology)" title="Sill (geology)">sills</a>, push upwards into the overlying rock, and crystallize as they intrude.
</p><p>After the initial sequence of rocks has been deposited, the rock units can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)" title="Deformation (mechanics)">deformed</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphosed</a>. Deformation typically occurs as a result of horizontal shortening, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extension_(geology)" title="Extension (geology)">horizontal extension</a>, or side-to-side (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strike-slip" title="Strike-slip">strike-slip</a>) motion. These structural regimes broadly relate to <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent boundaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundaries</a>, and transform boundaries, respectively, between tectonic plates.
</p><p>When rock units are placed under horizontal <a href="/wiki/Compression_(geology)" title="Compression (geology)">compression</a>, they shorten and become thicker. Because rock units, other than muds, <a href="/wiki/Incompressible_surface" title="Incompressible surface">do not significantly change in volume</a>, this is accomplished in two primary ways: through <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)" title="Fold (geology)">folding</a>. In the shallow crust, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brittle_deformation" title="Brittle deformation">brittle deformation</a> can occur, thrust faults form, which causes the deeper rock to move on top of the shallower rock. Because deeper rock is often older, as noted by the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_superposition" title="Law of superposition">principle of superposition</a>, this can result in older rocks moving on top of younger ones. Movement along faults can result in folding, either because the faults are not planar or because rock layers are dragged along, forming drag folds as slip occurs along the fault. Deeper in the Earth, rocks behave plastically and fold instead of faulting. These folds can either be those where the material in the center of the fold buckles upwards, creating "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiform" title="Antiform">antiforms</a>", or where it buckles downwards, creating "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synform" title="Synform">synforms</a>". If the tops of the rock units within the folds remain pointing upwards, they are called <a href="/wiki/Anticline" title="Anticline">anticlines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syncline" title="Syncline">synclines</a>, respectively. If some of the units in the fold are facing downward, the structure is called an overturned anticline or syncline, and if all of the rock units are overturned or the correct up-direction is unknown, they are simply called by the most general terms, antiforms, and synforms.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antecline_(PSF).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="609" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antecline_%28PSF%29.png/220px-Antecline_%28PSF%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antecline_%28PSF%29.png/330px-Antecline_%28PSF%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Antecline_%28PSF%29.png/440px-Antecline_%28PSF%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antecline_(PSF).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of folds, indicating an <a href="/wiki/Anticline" title="Anticline">anticline</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Syncline" title="Syncline">syncline</a></div></div></div>
<p>Even higher pressures and temperatures during horizontal shortening can cause both folding and <a href="/wiki/Metamorphism" title="Metamorphism">metamorphism</a> of the rocks. This metamorphism causes changes in the <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral composition</a> of the rocks; creates a <a href="/wiki/Foliation_(geology)" title="Foliation (geology)">foliation</a>, or planar surface, that is related to mineral growth under stress. This can remove signs of the original textures of the rocks, such as <a href="/wiki/Bed_(geology)" title="Bed (geology)">bedding</a> in sedimentary rocks, flow features of <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lavas</a>, and crystal patterns in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystalline_rock" title="Crystalline rock">crystalline rocks</a>.
</p><p>Extension causes the rock units as a whole to become longer and thinner. This is primarily accomplished through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_fault" title="Normal fault">normal faulting</a> and through the ductile stretching and thinning. Normal faults drop rock units that are higher below those that are lower. This typically results in younger units ending up below older units. Stretching of units can result in their thinning. In fact, at one location within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maria_Fold_and_Thrust_Belt" title="Maria Fold and Thrust Belt">Maria Fold and Thrust Belt</a>, the entire sedimentary sequence of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canyon" title="Grand Canyon">Grand Canyon</a> appears over a length of less than a meter. Rocks at the depth to be ductilely stretched are often also metamorphosed. These stretched rocks can also pinch into lenses, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Boudinage" title="Boudinage">boudins</a></i>, after the French word for "sausage" because of their visual similarity.
</p><p>Where rock units slide past one another, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strike-slip_fault" title="Strike-slip fault">strike-slip faults</a> develop in shallow regions, and become <a href="/wiki/Shear_zone" title="Shear zone">shear zones</a> at deeper depths where the rocks deform ductilely.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kittatinny_Mountain_Cross_Section.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Kittatinny_Mountain_Cross_Section.jpg/290px-Kittatinny_Mountain_Cross_Section.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Kittatinny_Mountain_Cross_Section.jpg 1.5x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kittatinny_Mountain_Cross_Section.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geological <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geology)" title="Cross section (geology)">cross section</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kittatinny_Mountain" title="Kittatinny Mountain">Kittatinny Mountain</a>. This cross-section shows metamorphic rocks, overlain by younger sediments deposited after the metamorphic event. These rock units were later folded and faulted during the uplift of the mountain.</div></div></div>
<p>The addition of new rock units, both depositionally and intrusively, often occurs during deformation. Faulting and other deformational processes result in the creation of topographic gradients, causing material on the rock unit that is increasing in elevation to be eroded by hillslopes and channels. These sediments are deposited on the rock unit that is going down. Continual motion along the fault maintains the topographic gradient in spite of the movement of sediment and continues to create <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accommodation_space" title="Accommodation space">accommodation space</a> for the material to deposit. Deformational events are often also associated with volcanism and igneous activity. Volcanic ashes and lavas accumulate on the surface, and igneous intrusions enter from below. <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">Dikes</a>, long, planar igneous intrusions, enter along cracks, and therefore often form in large numbers in areas that are being actively deformed. This can result in the emplacement of <a href="/wiki/Dike_swarm" title="Dike swarm">dike swarms</a>, such as those that are observable across the Canadian shield, or rings of dikes around the <a href="/wiki/Lava_tube" title="Lava tube">lava tube</a> of a volcano.
</p><p>All of these processes do not necessarily occur in a single environment and do not necessarily occur in a single order. The <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Islands" title="Hawaiian Islands">Hawaiian Islands</a>, for example, consist almost entirely of layered <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basaltic</a> lava flows. The sedimentary sequences of the mid-continental United States and the <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_Grand_Canyon_area" title="Geology of the Grand Canyon area">Grand Canyon</a> in the southwestern United States contain almost-undeformed stacks of sedimentary rocks that have remained in place since <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> time. Other areas are much more geologically complex. In the southwestern United States, sedimentary, volcanic, and intrusive rocks have been metamorphosed, faulted, foliated, and folded. Even older rocks, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acasta_gneiss" title="Acasta gneiss">Acasta gneiss</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_craton" title="Slave craton">Slave craton</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oldest_rock" title="Oldest rock">oldest known rock in the world</a> have been metamorphosed to the point where their origin is indiscernible without laboratory analysis. In addition, these processes can occur in stages. In many places, the Grand Canyon in the southwestern United States being a very visible example, the lower rock units were metamorphosed and deformed, and then deformation ended and the upper, undeformed units were deposited. Although any amount of rock emplacement and rock deformation can occur, and they can occur any number of times, these concepts provide a guide to understanding the <a href="/wiki/Historical_geology" title="Historical geology">geological history</a> of an area.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_geology">Methods of geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Methods of geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brunton.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2593" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Brunton.JPG/220px-Brunton.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Brunton.JPG/330px-Brunton.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Brunton.JPG/440px-Brunton.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brunton.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A standard <a href="/wiki/Brunton_compass" title="Brunton compass">Brunton Pocket Transit</a>, commonly used by geologists for mapping and surveying</div></div></div>
<p>Geologists use a number of fields, laboratory, and numerical modeling methods to decipher Earth history and to understand the processes that occur on and inside the Earth. In typical geological investigations, geologists use primary information related to <a href="/wiki/Petrology" title="Petrology">petrology</a> (the study of rocks), stratigraphy (the study of sedimentary layers), and structural geology (the study of positions of rock units and their deformation). In many cases, geologists also study modern soils, <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscapes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a>; investigate past and current life and <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemistry" title="Biogeochemistry">biogeochemical</a> pathways, and use <a href="/wiki/Geophysics" title="Geophysics">geophysical methods</a> to investigate the subsurface. Sub-specialities of geology may distinguish <b>endogenous</b> and <b>exogenous</b> geology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Field_methods">Field methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Field methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1661" data-file-width="2672" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg/220px-USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg/330px-USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg/440px-USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USGS_1950s_mapping_field_camp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">USGS</a> field mapping camp in the 1950s</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PDA_Mapping.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="302" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PDA_Mapping.jpg/220px-PDA_Mapping.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/PDA_Mapping.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PDA_Mapping.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Today, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handheld_computer" title="Handheld computer">handheld computers</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographic_information_systems" title="Geographic information systems">geographic information systems</a> software are often used in geological field work (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_geological_mapping" title="Digital geological mapping">digital geological mapping</a>).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1231" data-file-width="821" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/170px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/255px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg/340px-Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Petrified_forest_log_1_md.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrified" title="Petrified">petrified</a> log in <a href="/wiki/Petrified_Forest_National_Park" title="Petrified Forest National Park">Petrified Forest National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, U.S.A.</div></div></div>
<p>Geological <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_work" title="Field work">field work</a> varies depending on the task at hand. Typical fieldwork could consist of:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_map" title="Geological map">Geological mapping</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
<ul><li>Structural mapping: identifying the locations of major rock units and the faults and folds that led to their placement there.</li>
<li>Stratigraphic mapping: pinpointing the locations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary_facies" title="Sedimentary facies">sedimentary facies</a> (<a href="/wiki/Lithology" title="Lithology">lithofacies</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofacies" title="Biofacies">biofacies</a>) or the mapping of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopach" title="Isopach">isopachs</a> of equal thickness of sedimentary rock</li>
<li>Surficial mapping: recording the locations of soils and surficial deposits</li></ul></li>
<li>Surveying of topographic features
<ul><li>compilation of <a href="/wiki/Topographic_map" title="Topographic map">topographic maps</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li>Work to understand change across landscapes, including:
<ul><li>Patterns of <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">deposition</a></li>
<li>River-channel change through <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">migration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">avulsion</a></li>
<li>Hillslope processes</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>Subsurface mapping through <a href="/wiki/Geophysical_survey" title="Geophysical survey">geophysical methods</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
<ul><li>These methods include:
<ul><li>Shallow <a href="/wiki/Seismology" title="Seismology">seismic</a> surveys</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ground-penetrating_radar" title="Ground-penetrating radar">Ground-penetrating radar</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aeromagnetic_survey" title="Aeromagnetic survey">Aeromagnetic surveys</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Electrical_resistivity_tomography" title="Electrical resistivity tomography">Electrical resistivity tomography</a></li></ul></li>
<li>They aid in:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Exploration_geophysics" title="Exploration geophysics">Hydrocarbon exploration</a></li>
<li>Finding <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeological_geophysics" title="Archaeological geophysics">Locating buried archaeological artifacts</a></li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li>High-resolution stratigraphy
<ul><li>Measuring and describing stratigraphic sections on the surface</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Well_drilling" title="Well drilling">Well drilling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Well_logging" title="Well logging">logging</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biogeochemistry" title="Biogeochemistry">Biogeochemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geomicrobiology" title="Geomicrobiology">geomicrobiology</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
<ul><li>Collecting samples to:
<ul><li>determine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biochemical_pathway" title="Biochemical pathway">biochemical pathways</a></li>
<li>identify new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species_(biology)" title="Species (biology)">species</a> of organisms</li>
<li>identify new <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a></li></ul></li>
<li>and to use these discoveries to:
<ul><li>understand early life on Earth and how it functioned and metabolized</li>
<li>find important compounds for use in pharmaceuticals</li></ul></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">Paleontology</a>: excavation of <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> material
<ul><li>For research into past life and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a></li>
<li>For <a href="/wiki/Museum" title="Museum">museums</a> and education</li></ul></li>
<li>Collection of samples for <a href="/wiki/Geochronology" title="Geochronology">geochronology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermochronology" title="Thermochronology">thermochronology</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">Glaciology</a>: measurement of characteristics of glaciers and their motion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li></ul>
