The method is based on the distinct refractive indexes of different minerals.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petrology">Petrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Petrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petrology" title="Petrology">Petrology</a></div>
<p>In addition to identifying rocks in the field (<a href="/wiki/Lithology" title="Lithology">lithology</a>), petrologists identify rock samples in the laboratory. Two of the primary methods for identifying rocks in the laboratory are through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_microscopy" title="Optical microscopy">optical microscopy</a> and by using an <a href="/wiki/Electron_microprobe" title="Electron microprobe">electron microprobe</a>. In an <a href="/wiki/Optical_mineralogy" title="Optical mineralogy">optical mineralogy</a> analysis, petrologists analyze <a href="/wiki/Thin_section" title="Thin section">thin sections</a> of rock samples using a <a href="/wiki/Petrographic_microscope" title="Petrographic microscope">petrographic microscope</a>, where the minerals can be identified through their different properties in plane-polarized and cross-polarized light, including their <a href="/wiki/Birefringence" title="Birefringence">birefringence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pleochroism" title="Pleochroism">pleochroism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crystal_twinning" title="Crystal twinning">twinning</a>, and interference properties with a <a href="/wiki/Conoscopy" title="Conoscopy">conoscopic lens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the electron microprobe, individual locations are analyzed for their exact chemical compositions and variation in composition within individual crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">Stable</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> studies provide insight into the <a href="/wiki/Geochemistry" title="Geochemistry">geochemical</a> evolution of rock units.
</p><p>Petrologists can also use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluid_inclusions" title="Fluid inclusions">fluid inclusion</a> data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and perform high temperature and pressure physical experiments<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> to understand the temperatures and pressures at which different mineral phases appear, and how they change through igneous<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and metamorphic processes. This research can be extrapolated to the field to understand metamorphic processes and the conditions of crystallization of igneous rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This work can also help to explain processes that occur within the Earth, such as <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magma_chamber" title="Magma chamber">magma chamber</a> evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG/220px-Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG/330px-Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG/440px-Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Agiospavlos_DM_2004_IMG003_Felsenformation_nahe.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folded <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rock</a> strata</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_geology">Structural geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Structural geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structural_geology" title="Structural geology">Structural geology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orogenic_wedge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="213" data-file-width="580" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Orogenic_wedge.jpg/400px-Orogenic_wedge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/Orogenic_wedge.jpg 1.5x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orogenic_wedge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of an orogenic wedge. The wedge grows through faulting in the interior and along the main basal fault, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decollement" title="Decollement">décollement</a>. It builds its shape into a <a href="/wiki/Critical_taper" title="Critical taper">critical taper</a>, in which the angles within the wedge remain the same as failures inside the material balance failures along the décollement. It is analogous to a bulldozer pushing a pile of dirt, where the bulldozer is the overriding plate.</div></div></div>
<p>Structural geologists use microscopic analysis of oriented thin sections of geological samples to observe the <a href="/wiki/Fabric_(geology)" title="Fabric (geology)">fabric</a> within the rocks, which gives information about strain within the crystalline structure of the rocks. They also plot and combine measurements of geological structures to better understand the orientations of faults and folds to reconstruct the history of rock deformation in the area. In addition, they perform <a href="/wiki/Analogue_modelling_(geology)" title="Analogue modelling (geology)">analog</a> and numerical experiments of rock deformation in large and small settings.
</p><p>The analysis of structures is often accomplished by plotting the orientations of various features onto <a href="/wiki/Stereographic_projection" title="Stereographic projection">stereonets</a>. A stereonet is a stereographic projection of a sphere onto a plane, in which planes are projected as lines and lines are projected as points. These can be used to find the locations of fold axes, relationships between faults, and relationships between other geological structures.
</p><p>Among the most well-known experiments in structural geology are those involving <a href="/wiki/Accretionary_wedge" title="Accretionary wedge">orogenic wedges</a>, which are zones in which <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> are built along <a href="/wiki/Convergent_boundary" title="Convergent boundary">convergent</a> tectonic plate boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In the analog versions of these experiments, horizontal layers of sand are pulled along a lower surface into a back stop, which results in realistic-looking patterns of faulting and the growth of a <a href="/wiki/Critical_taper" title="Critical taper">critically tapered</a> (all angles remain the same) orogenic wedge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Numerical models work in the same way as these analog models, though they are often more sophisticated and can include patterns of erosion and uplift in the mountain belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This helps to show the relationship between erosion and the shape of a mountain range. These studies can also give useful information about pathways for metamorphism through pressure, temperature, space, and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratigraphy">Stratigraphy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Stratigraphy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ritagli_di_Lecca,_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG/220px-Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG/330px-Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG/440px-Ritagli_di_Lecca%2C_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ritagli_di_Lecca,_Fondachelli_Fantina.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different colors show the different minerals composing the mount Ritagli di Lecca seen from <a href="/wiki/Fondachelli-Fantina" title="Fondachelli-Fantina">Fondachelli-Fantina</a>, Sicily</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">Stratigraphy</a></div>
<p>In the laboratory, stratigraphers analyze samples of stratigraphic sections that can be returned from the field, such as those from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drill_core" title="Drill core">drill cores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodell_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-hodell-41">[41]</a></sup> Stratigraphers also analyze data from geophysical surveys that show the locations of stratigraphic units in the subsurface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Geophysical data and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Well_log" title="Well log">well logs</a> can be combined to produce a better view of the subsurface, and stratigraphers often use computer programs to do this in three dimensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Stratigraphers can then use these data to reconstruct ancient processes occurring on the surface of the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> interpret past environments, and locate areas for water, coal, and hydrocarbon extraction.
</p><p>In the laboratory, <a href="/wiki/Biostratigraphy" title="Biostratigraphy">biostratigraphers</a> analyze rock samples from outcrop and drill cores for the fossils found in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodell_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-hodell-41">[41]</a></sup> These fossils help scientists to date the core and to understand the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sedimentary_depositional_environment" title="Sedimentary depositional environment">depositional environment</a> in which the rock units formed. Geochronologists precisely date rocks within the stratigraphic section to provide better absolute bounds on the timing and rates of deposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
Magnetic stratigraphers look for signs of magnetic reversals in igneous rock units within the drill cores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodell_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-hodell-41">[41]</a></sup> Other scientists perform stable-isotope studies on the rocks to gain information about past climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hodell_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-hodell-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_geology">Planetary geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Planetary geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Viking_21i093.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="510" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mars_Viking_21i093.png/220px-Mars_Viking_21i093.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mars_Viking_21i093.png/330px-Mars_Viking_21i093.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Mars_Viking_21i093.png/440px-Mars_Viking_21i093.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mars_Viking_21i093.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Surface of Mars as photographed by the <a href="/wiki/Viking_2" title="Viking 2">Viking 2</a> lander December 9, 1977</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Planetary_geology" title="Planetary geology">Planetary geology</a></div>
<p>With the advent of <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a> in the twentieth century, geologists have begun to look at other planetary bodies in the same ways that have been developed to study the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. This new field of study is called <a href="/wiki/Planetary_geology" title="Planetary geology">planetary geology</a> (sometimes known as astrogeology) and relies on known geological principles to study other bodies of the solar system.
</p><p>Although the Greek-language-origin prefix <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/geo-" title="wikt:geo-">geo</a></i> refers to Earth, "geology" is often used in conjunction with the names of other planetary bodies when describing their composition and internal processes: examples are "the <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Mars" title="Geology of Mars">geology of Mars</a>" and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_geology" title="Lunar geology">Lunar geology</a>". Specialized terms such as <i>selenology</i> (studies of the Moon), <i>areology</i> (of Mars), etc., are also in use.
</p><p>Although planetary geologists are interested in studying all aspects of other planets, a significant focus is to search for evidence of past or present life on other worlds. This has led to many missions whose primary or ancillary purpose is to examine planetary bodies for evidence of life. One of these is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phoenix_lander" title="Phoenix lander">Phoenix lander</a>, which analyzed <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Martian</a> polar soil for water, chemical, and mineralogical constituents related to biological processes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applied_geology">Applied geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Applied geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1313" data-file-width="1156" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg/170px-Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg/255px-Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg/340px-Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panning_on_the_Mokelumne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Man panning for <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mokelumne" title="Mokelumne">Mokelumne</a>. <a href="/wiki/Harper%27s_Weekly" title="Harper's Weekly">Harper's Weekly</a>: How We Got Gold in California. 1860</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_geology">Economic geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Economic geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_geology" title="Economic geology">Economic geology</a></div>
<p>Economic geology is a branch of geology that deals with aspects of economic minerals that humankind uses to fulfill various needs. Economic minerals are those extracted profitably for various practical uses. Economic geologists help locate and manage the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a>, such as petroleum and coal, as well as mineral resources, which include metals such as iron, copper, and uranium.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining_geology">Mining geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mining geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">Mining</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mining_geology" title="Mining geology">Mining geology</a> consists of the extractions of mineral resources from the Earth. Some resources of economic interests include <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and many minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perlite" title="Perlite">perlite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeolites" title="Zeolites">zeolites</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">pumice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a>, as well as elements such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_geology">Petroleum geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Petroleum geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mudlogging.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mudlogging.JPG/220px-Mudlogging.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mudlogging.JPG/330px-Mudlogging.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Mudlogging.JPG/440px-Mudlogging.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mudlogging.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mud log in process, a common way to study the <a href="/wiki/Lithology" title="Lithology">lithology</a> when drilling oil wells</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_geology" title="Petroleum geology">Petroleum geology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Petroleum_geologist" title="Petroleum geologist">Petroleum geologists</a> study the locations of the subsurface of the Earth that can contain extractable hydrocarbons, especially <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>. Because many of these reservoirs are found in <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_basin" title="Sedimentary basin">sedimentary basins</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> they study the formation of these basins, as well as their sedimentary and tectonic evolution and the present-day positions of the rock units.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_geology">Engineering geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Engineering geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Engineering_geology" title="Engineering geology">Engineering geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_mechanics" title="Soil mechanics">Soil mechanics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geotechnical_engineering" title="Geotechnical engineering">Geotechnical engineering</a></div>
<p>Engineering geology is the application of geological principles to engineering practice for the purpose of assuring that the geological factors affecting the location, design, construction, operation, and maintenance of engineering works are properly addressed. Engineering geology is distinct from <a href="/wiki/Geological_engineering" title="Geological engineering">geological engineering</a>, particularly in North America.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3584" data-file-width="2856" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg/170px-New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg/255px-New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg/340px-New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_water_well_opens_in_Shant_Abak_DVIDS92609.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A child drinks water from a <a href="/wiki/Well" title="Well">well</a> built as part of a hydrogeological humanitarian project in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the field of <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a>, geological principles and analyses are used in order to ascertain the mechanical principles of the material on which structures are built. This allows tunnels to be built without collapsing, bridges and skyscrapers to be built with sturdy foundations, and buildings to be built that will not settle in clay and mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogeology" title="Hydrogeology">Hydrogeology</a></div>
<p>Geology and geological principles can be applied to various environmental problems such as <a href="/wiki/Stream_restoration" title="Stream restoration">stream restoration</a>, the restoration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brownfields" title="Brownfields">brownfields</a>, and the understanding of the interaction between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">natural habitat</a> and the geological environment. Groundwater hydrology, or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogeology" title="Hydrogeology">hydrogeology</a>, is used to locate groundwater,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton,_Pixie_A._1995_217_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton,_Pixie_A._1995_217-48">[48]</a></sup> which can often provide a ready supply of uncontaminated water and is especially important in arid regions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> and to monitor the spread of contaminants in groundwater wells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton,_Pixie_A._1995_217_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton,_Pixie_A._1995_217-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Paleoclimatology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</a></div>
<p>Geologists also obtain data through stratigraphy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreholes" title="Boreholes">boreholes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Core_sample" title="Core sample">core samples</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>. Ice cores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and sediment cores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> are used for paleoclimate reconstructions, which tell geologists about past and present temperature, precipitation, and <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a> across the globe. These datasets are our primary source of information on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_change" title="Global climate change">global climate change</a> outside of instrumental data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_hazards">Natural hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Natural hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GCRockfall.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/GCRockfall.JPG/220px-GCRockfall.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/GCRockfall.JPG/330px-GCRockfall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/GCRockfall.JPG/440px-GCRockfall.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GCRockfall.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rockfall in the Grand Canyon</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_hazard#Geological_hazards" title="Natural hazard">Natural hazard § Geological hazards</a></div>
<p>Geologists and geophysicists study natural hazards in order to enact safe <a href="/wiki/Building_code" title="Building code">building codes</a> and warning systems that are used to prevent loss of property and life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Examples of important natural hazards that are pertinent to geology (as opposed those that are mainly or only pertinent to meteorology) are:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Avalanche" title="Avalanche">Avalanches</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">Earthquakes</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">Floods</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">Landslides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Debris_flow" title="Debris flow">debris flows</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/River_channel_migration" title="River channel migration">River channel migration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avulsion_(river)" title="Avulsion (river)">avulsion</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rockfall" title="Rockfall">Rockfalls</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">Sinkholes</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Soil_liquefaction" title="Soil liquefaction">Soil liquefaction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Subsidence" title="Subsidence">Subsidence</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">Tsunamis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">Volcanoes</a></li></ul></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Geology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_geology" title="History of geology">History of geology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_geology" title="Timeline of geology">Timeline of geology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2871" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg/220px-Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg/330px-Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg/440px-Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geological_map_Britain_William_Smith_1815.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(geologist)" title="William Smith (geologist)">William Smith</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_map" title="Geological map">geological map</a> of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, and southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. Completed in 1815, it was the second national-scale geologic map, and by far the most accurate of its time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-map_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-map-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="failed on 'second map' (September 2019)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup></div></div></div>
<p>The study of the physical material of the Earth dates back at least to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> when <a href="/wiki/Theophrastus" title="Theophrastus">Theophrastus</a> (372–287 BCE) wrote the work <i><a href="/wiki/Theophrastus#On_stones" title="Theophrastus">Peri Lithon</a></i> (<i>On Stones</i>). During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> period, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> wrote in detail of the many minerals and metals, then in practical use – even correctly noting the origin of <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a>. Additionally, in the 4th century BCE <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> made critical observations of the slow rate of geological change. He observed the composition of the land and formulated a theory where the Earth changes at a slow rate and that these changes cannot be observed during one person's lifetime. Aristotle developed one of the first evidence-based concepts connected to the geological realm regarding the rate at which the Earth physically changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_al-Rayh%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Abū al-Rayhān al-Bīrūnī">Abu al-Rayhan al-Biruni</a> (973–1048 CE) was one of the earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> geologists, whose works included the earliest writings on the <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_India" title="Geology of India">geology of India</a>, hypothesizing that the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> was once a sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Drawing from Greek and Indian scientific literature that were not destroyed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Muslim conquests</a>, the Persian scholar <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina</a> (Avicenna, 981–1037) proposed detailed explanations for the formation of mountains, the origin of earthquakes, and other topics central to modern geology, which provided an essential foundation for the later development of the science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In China, the <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Shen_Kuo" title="Shen Kuo">Shen Kuo</a> (1031–1095) formulated a hypothesis for the process of land formation: based on his observation of fossil animal shells in a geological <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">stratum</a> in a mountain hundreds of miles from the ocean, he inferred that the land was formed by the erosion of the mountains and by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposition_(sediment)" title="Deposition (sediment)">deposition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Steno" title="Nicolas Steno">Nicolas Steno</a> (1638–1686) is credited with the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_superposition" title="Law of superposition">law of superposition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_original_horizontality" title="Principle of original horizontality">principle of original horizontality</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_lateral_continuity" title="Principle of lateral continuity">principle of lateral continuity</a>: three defining principles of <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">stratigraphy</a>.
</p><p>The word <i>geology</i> was first used by <a href="/wiki/Ulisse_Aldrovandi" title="Ulisse Aldrovandi">Ulisse Aldrovandi</a> in 1603,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> then by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Andr%C3%A9_Deluc" title="Jean-André Deluc">Jean-André Deluc</a> in 1778<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and introduced as a fixed term by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horace-B%C3%A9n%C3%A9dict_de_Saussure" title="Horace-Bénédict de Saussure">Horace-Bénédict de Saussure</a> in 1779.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The word is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> γῆ, <i>gê</i>, meaning "earth" and λόγος, <i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i>, meaning "speech".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> But according to another source, the word "geology" comes from a Norwegian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mikkel_Peders%C3%B8n_Escholt" title="Mikkel Pedersøn Escholt">Mikkel Pedersøn Escholt</a> (1600–1699), who was a priest and scholar. Escholt first used the definition in his book titled, <i>Geologia Norvegica</i> (1657).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(geologist)" title="William Smith (geologist)">William Smith</a> (1769–1839) drew some of the first geological maps and began the process of ordering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_strata" title="Rock strata">rock strata</a> (layers) by examining the fossils contained in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-map_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-map-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1763, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Lomonosov" title="Mikhail Lomonosov">Mikhail Lomonosov</a> published his treatise <i>On the Strata of Earth</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> His work was the first narrative of modern geology, based on the unity of processes in time and explanation of the Earth's past from the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a> (1726-1797) is often viewed as the first modern geologist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In 1785 he presented a paper entitled <i>Theory of the Earth</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh" title="Royal Society of Edinburgh">Royal Society of Edinburgh</a>. In his paper, he explained his theory that the Earth must be much older than had previously been supposed to allow enough time for mountains to be eroded and for <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediments</a> to form new rocks at the bottom of the sea, which in turn were raised up to become dry land. Hutton published a two-volume version of his ideas in 1795.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Followers of Hutton were known as <i><a href="/wiki/Plutonism" title="Plutonism">Plutonists</a></i> because they believed that some rocks were formed by <i>vulcanism</i>, which is the deposition of lava from volcanoes, as opposed to the <i><a href="/wiki/Neptunism" title="Neptunism">Neptunists</a></i>, led by <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Gottlob_Werner" title="Abraham Gottlob Werner">Abraham Werner</a>, who believed that all rocks had settled out of a large ocean whose level gradually dropped over time.
</p><p>The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_map_of_Georgia" title="Geologic map of Georgia">geological map of the U.S.</a> was produced in 1809 by <a href="/wiki/William_Maclure" title="William Maclure">William Maclure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maclure1817_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maclure1817-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1807, Maclure commenced the self-imposed task of making a geological survey of the United States. Almost every state in the Union was traversed and mapped by him, the <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_Mountains" title="Allegheny Mountains">Allegheny Mountains</a> being crossed and recrossed some 50 times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The results of his unaided labours were submitted to the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> in a memoir entitled <i>Observations on the Geology of the United States explanatory of a Geological Map</i>, and published in the <i>Society's Transactions</i>, together with the nation's first geological map.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This antedates <a href="/wiki/William_Smith_(geologist)" title="William Smith (geologist)">William Smith</a>'s geological map of England by six years, although it was constructed using a different classification of rocks.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Charles_Lyell" title="Sir Charles Lyell">Sir Charles Lyell</a> (1797-1875) first published his famous book, <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Geology" title="Principles of Geology">Principles of Geology</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> in 1830. This book, which influenced the thought of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>, successfully promoted the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Uniformitarianism" title="Uniformitarianism">uniformitarianism</a>. This theory states that slow geological processes have occurred throughout the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">Earth's history</a> and are still occurring today. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Catastrophism" title="Catastrophism">catastrophism</a> is the theory that Earth's features formed in single, catastrophic events and remained unchanged thereafter. Though Hutton believed in uniformitarianism, the idea was not widely accepted at the time.
</p><p>Much of 19th-century geology revolved around the question of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">Earth's exact age</a>. Estimates varied from a few hundred thousand to billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> By the early 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a> allowed the Earth's age to be estimated at two billion years. The awareness of this vast amount of time opened the door to new theories about the processes that shaped the planet.
</p><p>Some of the most significant advances in 20th-century geology have been the development of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> in the 1960s and the refinement of estimates of the planet's age. Plate tectonics theory arose from two separate geological observations: <a href="/wiki/Seafloor_spreading" title="Seafloor spreading">seafloor spreading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_drift" title="Continental drift">continental drift</a>. The theory revolutionized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_sciences" title="Earth sciences">Earth sciences</a>. Today the Earth is known to be approximately 4.5 billion years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4.54_book_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-4.54_book-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFVai,_Gian_BattistaCavazza,_William2003">Vai, Gian Battista; Cavazza, William (2003). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Ip-rAAAACAAJ" rel="nofollow"><i>Four centuries of the word geology: Ulisse Aldrovandi 1603 in Bologna</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Deluc, Jean André de, <i>Lettres physiques et morales sur les montagnes et sur l'histoire de la terre et de l'homme. …</i> [Physical and moral letters on mountains and on the history of the Earth and man. … ], vol. 1 (Paris, France:  V. Duchesne, 1779), pp. 4, 5, and 7.  <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=20" rel="nofollow">From p. 4:</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181122140010/https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=20" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2018-11-22 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> <i>"Entrainé par les liaisons de cet objet avec la Géologie, j'entrepris dans un second voyage de les développer à SA MAJESTÉ; … "</i> (Driven by the connections between this subject and geology, I undertook a second voyage to develop them for Her Majesty [viz, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_of_Mecklenburg-Strelitz" title="Charlotte of Mecklenburg-Strelitz">Charlotte of Mecklenburg-Strelitz</a>, Queen of Great Britain and Ireland]; … )  <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=21" rel="nofollow">From p. 5:</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181122141605/https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=21" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2018-11-22 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> <i>"Je vis que je faisais un Traité, et non une equisse de </i>Géologie<i>."</i>  (I see that I wrote a treatise, and not a sketch of geology.)  <a class="external text" href="https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=23" rel="nofollow">From the footnote on p. 7:</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20181122141258/https://babel.hathitrust.org/cgi/pt?id=mdp.39015067148760;view=1up;seq=23" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2018-11-22 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> <i>"Je répète ici, ce que j'avois dit dans ma première </i>Préface<i>, sur la substitution de mot </i>Cosmologie<i> à celui de </i>Géologie<i>, quoiqu'il ne s'agisse pas de l'Univers, mais seulement de la </i>Terre<i>: … "</i>  (I repeat here what I said in my first preface about the substitution of the word "cosmology" for that of "geology", although it is not a matter of the universe but only of the Earth: … ) [Note:  A pirated edition of this book was published in 1778.]</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Saussure, Horace-Bénédict de, <i>Voyages dans les Alpes</i>, … (Neuchatel, (Switzerland):  Samuel Fauche, 1779).  <a class="external text" href="http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k102951m/f4" rel="nofollow">From pp. i–ii:</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170206185724/http://gallica.bnf.fr/ark:/12148/bpt6k102951m/f4" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2017-02-06 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> <i>"La science qui rassemble les faits, qui seuls peuvent servir de base à la Théorie de la Terre ou à la </i>Géologie<i>, c'est la Géographie physique, ou la description de notre Globe; … "</i> (The science that assembles the facts which alone can serve as the basis of the theory of the Earth or of "geology", is physical geography, or the description of our globe; … )</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the controversy regarding whether Deluc or Saussure deserves priority in the use the term "geology":

Ogilvie-Gordon, trans., <i>History of Geology and Paleontology to the End of the Nineteenth Century</i> (London, England:  Walter Scott, 1901), <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/stream/cu31924012130534#page/n95/mode/2up" rel="nofollow">p. 76.</a></li>

<div class="navbox-styles nomobile"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1061467846" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></div><div aria-labelledby="Geologic_principles_and_processes" class="navbox" role="navigation" style="padding:3px"><table class="nowraplinks mw-collapsible autocollapse navbox-inner" style="border-spacing:0;background:transparent;color:inherit"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-title" colspan="3" scope="col"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Geologic_Principles" title="Template:Geologic Principles"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;box-shadow:none;padding:0;" title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Geologic_Principles" title="Template talk:Geologic Principles"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;box-shadow:none;padding:0;" title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Geologic_Principles&amp;action=edit"><abbr style=";;background:none transparent;border:none;box-shadow:none;padding:0;" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div><div id="Geologic_principles_and_processes" style="font-size:114%;margin:0 4em"><a class="mw-selflink selflink"> Geologic</a> principles and processes</div></th></tr><tr><th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">Stratigraphic principles</a></th><td class="navbox-list-with-group navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0"><div style="padding:0 0.25em">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Principle_of_original_horizontality" title="Principle of original horizontality">Principle of original horizontality</a></li>
